<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2016 48:6"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-05-09"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Philipp Simon, Massimo Iorizzo, Allen Van Deynze and colleagues report the high-quality assembly of the carrot genome, providing an important resource for crop improvement. They find a candidate gene that regulates carotenoid accumulation and gain further insights into asterid genome evolution, including characterization of two new polyploidization events. We report a high-quality chromosome-scale assembly and analysis of the carrot (Daucus carota) genome, the first sequenced genome to include a comparative evolutionary analysis among members of the euasterid II clade. We characterized two new polyploidization events, both occurring after the divergence of carrot from members of the Asterales order, clarifying the evolutionary scenario before and after radiation of the two main asterid clades. Large- and small-scale lineage-specific duplications have contributed to the expansion of gene families, including those with roles in flowering time, defense response, flavor, and pigment accumulation. We identified a candidate gene, DCAR_032551, that conditions carotenoid accumulation (Y) in carrot taproot and is coexpressed with several isoprenoid biosynthetic genes. The primary mechanism regulating carotenoid accumulation in carrot taproot is not at the biosynthetic level. We hypothesize that DCAR_032551 regulates upstream photosystem development and functional processes, including photomorphogenesis and root de-etiolation."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>We report a high-quality chromosome-scale assembly and analysis of the carrot (<i>Daucus carota</i>) genome, the first sequenced genome to include a comparative evolutionary analysis among members of the euasterid II clade. We characterized two new polyploidization events, both occurring after the divergence of carrot from members of the Asterales order, clarifying the evolutionary scenario before and after radiation of the two main asterid clades. Large- and small-scale lineage-specific duplications have contributed to the expansion of gene families, including those with roles in flowering time, defense response, flavor, and pigment accumulation. We identified a candidate gene, DCAR_032551, that conditions carotenoid accumulation (<i>Y</i>) in carrot taproot and is coexpressed with several isoprenoid biosynthetic genes. The primary mechanism regulating carotenoid accumulation in carrot taproot is not at the biosynthetic level. We hypothesize that DCAR_032551 regulates upstream photosystem development and functional processes, including photomorphogenesis and root de-etiolation.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Carrot (<i>Daucus carota</i> subsp. <i>carota</i> L.; 2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 18) is a globally important root crop whose production has quadrupled between 1976 and 2013 (FAO Statistics; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>), outpacing the overall rate of increase in vegetable production and world population growth (FAO Statistics; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>) through development of high-value products for fresh consumption, juices, and natural pigments and cultivars adapted to warmer production regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Simon, P.W. et al. in Carrot. Handbook of Plant Breeding, Vegetables II (eds. Prohens, J. &amp; Nuez, F.) 327–357 (Springer, 2008)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d2836e798">1</a></sup>.</p><p>The first documented colors for domesticated carrot root were yellow and purple in Central Asia approximately 1,100 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Zagorodskikh, P. New data on the origin and taxonomy of the cultivated carrot. C.R. (Doklady) Acad. Sci. USSR 25, 522–525 (1939)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d2836e805">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. Genetic structure and domestication of carrot (Daucus carota subsp. sativus) (Apiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 930–938 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2836e808">3</a></sup>, with orange carrots not reliably reported until the sixteenth century in Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Simon, P.W. Domestication, historical development, and modern breeding of carrot. Plant Breed. Rev. 19, 157–190 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2836e812">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Arscott, S.A. &amp; Tanumihardjo, S.A. Carrots of many colors provide basic nutrition and bioavailable phytochemicals acting as a functional food. Compr. Rev. Food Sci. Food Saf. 9, 223–239 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d2836e815">5</a></sup>. The popularity of orange carrots is fortuitous for modern consumers because the orange pigmentation results from high quantities of alpha- and beta-carotene, making carrots the richest source of provitamin A in the US diet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Simon, P.W. Plant breeding for human nutritional quality. Plant Breed. Rev. 31, 325–392 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2836e819">6</a></sup>. Carrot breeding has substantially increased nutritional value, with a 50% average increase in carotene content in the United States as compared to 40 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Simon, P.W. Plant breeding for human nutritional quality. Plant Breed. Rev. 31, 325–392 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2836e823">6</a></sup>. Lycopene and lutein in red and yellow carrots, respectively, are also nutritionally important carotenoids, making carrot a model system to study storage root development and carotenoid accumulation.</p><p>Carrot is the most important crop in the Apiaceae family, which includes numerous other vegetables, herbs, spices, and medicinal plants that enhance the epicurean experience<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Rubatzky, V.E., Quiros, C.F. &amp; Simon, P.W. Carrots and Related Vegetable Umbelliferae (CABI, 1999)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d2836e830">7</a></sup>, including celery, parsnip, arracacha, parsley, fennel, coriander, and cumin. The Apiaceae family belongs to the euasterid II clade, which includes important crops such as lettuce and sunflower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bremer, B. in Asterids.The Timetree of Life (eds. Hedges, S.B. &amp; Kumar, S.) 177–178 (Oxford University Press, 2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2836e834">8</a></sup>. Genome sequences of euasterid I species have been reported, but only two genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Peng, Y. et al. De novo genome assembly of the economically important weed horseweed using integrated data from multiple sequencing platforms. Plant Physiol. 166, 1241–1254 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2836e838">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d2836e841">10</a></sup> have been published among the other euasterid II species.</p><p>Here we report a high-quality genome assembly of a doubled-haploid orange carrot, characterization of the mechanism controlling carotenoid accumulation in storage roots, and the resequencing of 35 accessions spanning the genetic diversity of the <i>Daucus</i> genus. Our comprehensive genomic analyses provide insights into the evolution of the asterids and several gene families. These results will facilitate biological discovery and crop improvement in carrot and other crops.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>An orange, doubled-haploid, Nantes-type carrot (DH1) was used for genome sequencing. We used BAC end sequences and a newly developed linkage map with 2,075 markers to correct 135 scaffolds with one or more chimeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">Supplementary Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">2</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>The resulting v2.0 assembly spans 421.5 Mb and contains 4,907 scaffolds (N50 of 12.7 Mb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Tab1">Table 1</a>), accounting for <span class="stix">∼</span>90% of the estimated genome size (473 Mb; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E.D. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208–218 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d2836e881">11</a></sup>. The scaftig N50 of 31.2 kb is similar to those of other high-quality genome assemblies such as potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Potato Genome Sequencing Consortium. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189–195 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d2836e885">12</a></sup> and pepper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kim, S. et al. Genome sequence of the hot pepper provides insights into the evolution of pungency in Capsicum species. Nat. Genet. 46, 270–278 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d2836e889">13</a></sup>. About 86% (362 Mb) of the assembled genome is included in only 60 superscaffolds anchored to the nine pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The longest superscaffold spans 30.2 Mb, 85% of chromosome 4.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the carrot genome and gene prediction</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3565/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In mapping of unassembled Illumina reads against the assembled genome, 99.7% of the reads aligned (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 3</a>), suggesting that the unassembled fraction of the carrot genome (<span class="stix">∼</span>10%) likely consists of assembled duplicated sequences. No substantial sequence contamination was detected (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). In mapping of carrot ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the carrot transcriptome reveals novel genes, new markers, and genetic diversity. BMC Genomics 12, 389 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1280">14</a></sup>, genes identified from transcriptome analysis in 20 unique DH1 tissue types, and 248 ultraconserved genes from the Core Eukaryotic Genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1284">15</a></sup> data set, <span class="stix">∼</span>94%, 98%, and 99.9% aligned to the carrot genome assembly, respectively, demonstrating that the assembly covers the majority of gene space (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 4–6</a>).</p><p>Mapping of 99.9% of 454 paired-end reads and 95.6% of paired-end BAC reads, within their estimated fragment lengths (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 7</a>), confirmed an accurate assembly. A linkage map including 394 markers aligned with high collinearity to 36 superscaffolds (covering 343.5 Mb) demonstrates correct ordering and orientation of these superscaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">Supplementary Figs. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">5</a>).</p><p>Cytological evaluations using subtelomeric BAC clones and a telomeric probe indicated that the assembly extends into telomeric and subtelomeric regions, further supporting the high physical coverage of the carrot genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Carrot chromosome 1 multidimensional topography and tandem repeat evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Carrot chromosome 1 multidimensional topography and tandem repeat evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="559"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The integrated linkage map for carrot is shown to the far left (the vertical bar to the left indicates genetic distance in cM). Lines connect a subset of markers to the pseudomolecule. Next, from left to right, are shown the cM/Mb ratio, predicted genes (percent of nucleotides/200-kb window), transcriptomes (percent of nucleotides/200-kb window), class I and class II repetitive sequences (percent of nucleotides/200-kb window), noncoding RNAs (percent of nucleotides/200-kb window), and SNPs (number of SNPs/100-kb window). Genes and TEs are more abundant in the distal and pericentromeric regions of the chromosomes, respectively. DNA pseudomolecules are shown in orange to the right. Gray horizontal lines indicate gaps between superscaffolds. Horizontal blue and red lines labeled on the right indicate the locations of BAC probes hybridized to pachytene chromosome 1 (see <b>b</b>); a horizontal yellow line indicates the location of the telomeric repeats. To the far right is a digitally straightened representation of carrot chromosome 1 probed with oligonucleotide probes to the telomeric repeats (Telo; blue) and the CL80 and Cent-Dc repeats (red) and with probes corresponding to BAC 68M03 (red) specific to chromosome 1 and BACs 20G08 and 20P12 (green) flanking the CL80 repeat. (<b>b</b>) FISH mapping of oligonucleotide probes to telomeric repeats (Telo; yellow) and the CL80 repeat (red) and probes corresponding to BAC clones specific to the termini of the short (1S; green) and long (1L; red) arms of carrot chromosome 1. (<b>c</b>–<b>e</b>) FISH mapping of the CL80 (red) and Cent-Dc (K11; green) repeats on the pachytene complements of DH1 (<b>c</b>), <i>D. guttatus</i> (<b>d</b>), and <i>Daucus littoralis</i> (<b>e</b>). Cent-Dc did not generate any detectable signals in <i>D. guttatus</i> or <i>D. littoralis</i>. Scale bars, 5 μm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Together, the assembly statistics and corroborating evaluations demonstrate that the assembly achieved standard parameters of high quality<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Chain, P.S. et al. Genome project standards in a new era of sequencing. Science 326, 236–237 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1374">16</a></sup>. On the basis of genome coverage and length of sequence contiguity, the carrot genome assembly is one of the most complete genomes reported (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome characterization</h3><p>Carrot coding regions, tandem repeats, and mobile elements were characterized to evaluate the structural and functional features contributing to carrot evolution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>). Repetitive sequences accounted for 46% of the genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Tab1">Table 1</a>), of which 98% (193.7 Mb) were annotated as transposable elements (TEs) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Class II TEs accounted for 57.4 Mb—a greater amount of the genome than in similarly sized plant genomes, including rice (48 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1398">17</a></sup>. Given the abundance of class II TEs, we studied the evolution and distribution of insertion sites for two miniature inverted-repeat transposable element (MITE) class II families, <i>Tourist</i>-like <i>Krak</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Grzebelus, D., Yau, Y.-Y. &amp; Simon, P.W. Master: A novel family of PIF/Harbinger-like transposable elements identified in carrot (Daucus carota L.). Mol. Genet. Genomics 275, 450–459 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1408">18</a></sup> and <i>Stowaway</i>-like <i>DcSto</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Macko-Podgorni, A., Nowicka, A., Grzebelus, E., Simon, P.W. &amp; Grzebelus, D. DcSto: carrot Stowaway-like elements are abundant, diverse, and polymorphic. Genetica 141, 255–267 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1417">19</a></sup>. The expansion of <i>DcSto</i> elements was characterized by multiple amplification bursts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Over 50% of <i>DcSto</i> and <i>Krak</i> insertion sites were located near (&lt;2 kb away from) or inside predicted genes. However, no evidence was found to support their preferential insertion in genic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>), supporting the hypothesis that the impact of DNA transposons on gene function and genome evolution may reflect the interplay of stochastic events and selective pressure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Santiago, N., Herráiz, C., Goñi, J.R., Messeguer, X. &amp; Casacuberta, J.M. Genome-wide analysis of the Emigrant family of MITEs of Arabidopsis thaliana. Mol. Biol. Evol. 19, 2285–2293 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1437">20</a></sup>.</p><p>Tandem repeat–rich regions create a technical challenge to genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Miga, K.H. et al. Centromere reference models for human chromosomes X and Y satellite arrays. Genome Res. 24, 697–707 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1444">21</a></sup>. By using RepeatExplorer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Novák, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792–793 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1448">22</a></sup> and cytology, we identified four major tandem repeat families accounting for <span class="stix">∼</span>7% of the DH1 genome and traced their evolutionary history in the <i>Daucus</i> genus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 10</a>). These tandem repeats included the carrot centromeric satellite Cent-Dc (CL1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Iovene, M. et al. Comparative FISH mapping of Daucus species (Apiaceae family). Chromosome Res. 19, 493–506 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1458">23</a></sup> and three new tandem repeats (CL8, CL80, and CL81). In DH1 and related species, 39- to 40-bp Cent-Dc monomers were organized in a higher-order repeat structure (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). <i>Daucus</i> species distantly related to carrot were enriched for the CL80 repeat, which occupied most subtelomeric and pericentromeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM297">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). Conversely, the carrot CL80 sequence was associated with a knob on chromosome 1. Because Cent-Dc and CL80 were detected in members of the divergent <i>Daucus</i> clades (<i>Daucus</i> I and II), we hypothesize that their origin predates the estimated divergence of the two clades <span class="stix">∼</span>20 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Spalik, K. et al. Amphitropic amphiantarctic disjunctions in Apiaceae subfamily Apioideae. J. Biogeogr. 37, 1977–1994 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1482">24</a></sup>. After <i>Daucus</i> radiated, these repeat families presumably underwent differential expansion and shrinkage of their repeat arrays and structural reorganization of monomers.</p><p>In assembly v1.0 gene annotation, 32,113 genes were predicted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>), of which 79% had substantial homology with known genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 11 and 12</a>). The majority (98.7%) of gene predictions had supporting cDNA and/or EST evidence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 13</a>), demonstrating the high accuracy of gene prediction. Relative to five other closely related genomes, carrot was enriched for genes involved in a wide range of molecular functions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 14</a>). We also identified 564 tRNAs, 31 rRNA fragments, 532 small nuclear RNA (snRNA) genes, and 248 microRNAs (miRNAs) distributed among 46 families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 15</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Carrot diversity analysis</h3><p>To evaluate carrot domestication patterns, we resequenced 35 carrot accessions, representative <i>D. carota</i> subspecies, and outgroups (<i>Daucus syrticus</i>, <i>Daucus sahariensis</i>, <i>Daucus aureus</i>, and <i>Daucus guttatus</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 16</a>). After filtering, 1,393,431 high-quality SNPs (accuracy &gt;95%; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>) were identified, with the largest number of diverging or alternate alleles in outgroups, a signature of genome divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 17</a>). Phylogenetic and cluster analysis separated samples by geographical distribution relative to carrot's Central Asian center of origin (eastern or western) and cultivation status (wild, cultivated, open pollinated, or inbred) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). Eastern wild accessions were most closely related to cultivated carrots, further demonstrating a primary center of carrot domestication in the Middle East and Central Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. Genetic structure and domestication of carrot (Daucus carota subsp. sativus) (Apiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 930–938 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1550">3</a></sup>. Cluster analysis showed extensive allelic admixture (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>), reflective of the outcrossing nature within carrot combined with extensive geographical overlap between wild and cultivated carrot lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Simon, P.W. Domestication, historical development, and modern breeding of carrot. Plant Breed. Rev. 19, 157–190 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1558">4</a></sup>. This pattern was particularly evident in eastern wild and cultivated samples, likely caused by less intensive carrot breeding in eastern regions. Indeed, some eastern cultivated carrots still maintain primary taproot lateral branching and reduced pigmentation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). In contrast, western cultivars clearly separated from wild and eastern cultivated carrots, and some inbred lines (I3 and I4) have a purified genetic pattern shared with western cultivated accessions, reflecting the intensive breeding practiced in western regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Carrot genetic diversity."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Carrot genetic diversity.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1083"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Top, neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree of carrot and other <i>Daucus</i> accessions based on SNPs. Bottom, population structure of <i>Daucus</i> accessions. Each color represents a subpopulation, and each accession is represented by a vertical bar. The length of the colored segments in the vertical bars represents the proportion contributed by each subpopulation. (<b>b</b>) The 26 inner tracks depict the SNP frequency distributions for 100-kb non-overlapping windows in the 8 wild <i>D. carota</i> subsp. <i>carota</i> accessions (blue tracks), 14 open-pollinated cultivars and local land races (green tracks), and 4 inbred lines (orange tracks). The outermost track shows the positions of SNPs with the top 1% of <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values, estimated by comparing SNPs from wild and cultivated eastern accessions. The track below this shows the location of markers spanning the QTLs associated with the <i>Y</i> (chromosome 5) and <i>Y</i><sub>2</sub> (chromosome 7) loci<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Buishand, J.G. &amp; Gabelman, W.H. Investigations on the inheritance of color and carotenoid content in phloem and xylem of carrot roots (Daucus carota L.). Euphytica 28, 611–632 (1979)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1607">40</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Nei, M. &amp; Li, W.-H. Mathematical model for studying genetic variation in terms of restriction endonucleases. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 76, 5269–5273 (1979)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1625">25</a></sup> estimates showed that wild carrots have a slightly higher level of genetic diversity than cultivated carrots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 18</a>), indicating the occurrence of a limited domestication bottleneck, consistent with previous findings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. Genetic structure and domestication of carrot (Daucus carota subsp. sativus) (Apiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 930–938 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1632">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Rong, J. et al. New insights into domestication of carrot from root transcriptome analyses. BMC Genomics 15, 895 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1635">26</a></sup>. When <i>D. carota</i> subspecies, which have morphological characteristics contributing to their sexual isolation relative to carrot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Arbizu, C., Reitsma, K.R., Simon, P.W. &amp; Spooner, D.M. Morphometrics of Daucus (Apiaceae): a counterpart to a phylogenomic study. Am. J. Bot. 101, 2005–2016 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1643">27</a></sup>, were excluded from diversity estimates, this observation was more evident from comparative analysis (wild, <i>π</i> = 9.5 × 10<sup>−4</sup> versus cultivated, <i>π</i> = 8.6 × 10<sup>−4</sup>). In contrast, a clear reduction in genetic diversity and heterozygosity was found in inbred lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 17</a>), likely resulting from their use in hybrid carrot breeding programs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Welch, J.E. &amp; Grimball, E.L. Jr. Male sterility in the carrot. Science 106, 594 (1947)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1664">28</a></sup>.</p><p>To identify genomic regions associated with domestication events, we computed pairwise population differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) levels for wild and cultivated eastern accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Weir, B.S. &amp; Cockerham, C.C. Estimating F-statistics for the analysis of population structure. Evolution 38, 1358 (1984)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1675">29</a></sup>, as these samples resemble the genetic pool for primary carrot domestication. We identified local differentiation signals on chromosomes 2, 5, 6, 7, and 8. Peaks on chromosomes 5 and 7 overlap with previously mapped quantitative trait loci (QTLs) controlling carotenoid accumulation in tap root (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), a major domestication trait in carrot.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome evolution</h3><p>Comparative phylogenomic analysis among 13 plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>) indicated that carrot diverged from grape <span class="stix">∼</span>113 million years ago, from kiwifruit <span class="stix">∼</span>101 million years ago, and from potato and tomato <span class="stix">∼</span>90.5 million years ago, confirming the previously estimated dating of the asterid crown group to the Early Cretaceous and its radiation in the Late–Early Cretaceous<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bremer, B. in Asterids.The Timetree of Life (eds. Hedges, S.B. &amp; Kumar, S.) 177–178 (Oxford University Press, 2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1696">8</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). Further divergence between carrot and lettuce, both members of the euasterid II clade, likely occurred <span class="stix">∼</span>72 million years ago.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Carrot genome evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Carrot genome evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="571"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Evolutionary relationships of the eudicot lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). Circles indicate the ages of WGD (red) or WGT (blue) events. Age estimates for the <i>A. thaliana</i>, kiwifruit, lettuce, and Solanaceae WGD and WGT events and for the γ WGT event were obtained from the literature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1728">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1731">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Jiao, Y. et al. A genome triplication associated with early diversification of the core eudicots. Genome Biol. 13, R3 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1734">66</a></sup>. The polyploidization level of the kiwifruit WGDs (purple circles) awaits confirmation. Mya, million years ago. (<b>b</b>) Age distribution of fourfold-degenerate sites for genes from the <i>D. carota</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> genomes. The <i>x</i> axis shows fourfold-degenerate transversion rates; the <i>y</i> axis shows the percentage of gene pairs in syntenic or collinear blocks. The γ peak represents the ancestral γ WGT shared by core eudicots; Dc-α and Dc-β represent carrot-specific WGD and WGT events, respectively. (<b>c</b>) The distribution of remaining carrot duplicated blocks derived from the seven eudicot protochromosomes. (<b>d</b>) Synteny of carrot protochromosome A19 with corresponding blocks on grape, coffee, tomato, and kiwifruit chromosomes. Vertical bars indicate the depth of primary correspondence to carrot protochromosome A19. Of the 110 syntenic blocks identified in comparison of carrot and grape protochromosome A19, a substantial portion (43; 39.1%) correspond to 6 grape blocks. A similar pattern was observed for the carrot–coffee, carrot–kiwifruit, and carrot–tomato comparisons, indicating that carrot has experienced either 3 × 2 or 2 × 3 WGD events. (<b>e</b>) Representation of carrot-specific genome duplications. The tracks, from outermost to innermost, show GC content (%), density of tandem duplications (number per 0.5-Mb window), genes retained in the carrot Dc-α (cyan) and Dc-β (blue) events, chromosomal blocks descending from the seven ancestral core eudicot protochromosomes (colored as in <b>c</b>), and duplicated segments derived from the Dc-α (dashed links; duplicates) and Dc-β (solid links; triplicates) events.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified two new whole-genome duplications (WGDs) specific to the carrot lineage, Dc-α and Dc-β, superimposed on the earlier γ paleohexaploidy event shared by all eudicots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig3">Fig. 3a,b</a>). These WGDs likely occurred <span class="stix">∼</span>43 and <span class="stix">∼</span>70 million years ago, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Estimating the timing of the Dc-β WGD to around the Cretaceous–Paleogene (K–Pg) boundary further supports the hypothesis that a WGD burst occurred around that time, perhaps reflecting a selective polyploid advantage in comparison to diploid progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1787">30</a></sup>. These results may also suggest a co-occurrence of the Dc-β WGD with Apiales–Asterales divergence. To address this possibility, we compared the carrot genome with the genome of horseweed (<i>Conyza canadensis</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>), an Asteraceae with a low-pass whole-genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Peng, Y. et al. De novo genome assembly of the economically important weed horseweed using integrated data from multiple sequencing platforms. Plant Physiol. 166, 1241–1254 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1798">9</a></sup>. Pairwise paralog and ortholog gene divergence indicated that a possible WGD occurred in the horseweed genome that does not overlap with the carrot Dc-β event, as it occurred after divergence with carrot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). This WGD is likely shared with lettuce and may represent a whole-genome triplication (WGT) recently described in lettuce that is basal to Asteraceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Truco, M.J. et al. An ultra-high-density, transcript-based, genetic map of lettuce. G3 3, 617–631 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1805">31</a></sup>.</p><p>Using methods previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1812">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Salse, J. et al. Reconstruction of monocotelydoneous proto-chromosomes reveals faster evolution in plants than in animals. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14908–14913 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1815">33</a></sup>, we reconstructed the carrot paleopolyploidy history. Carrot chromosomal blocks descending from the seven ancestral core eudicot chromosomes were highly fragmented and dispersed along the nine carrot chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). The two lineage-specific WGDs were clearly evident from the distribution of the fourfold-degenerate transversion rates of carrot paleohexaploid paralogous genes, whereas genes from the shared eudicot γ WGT were largely lost, likely owing to extensive genome fractionation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Comparative analysis with the grape, tomato, coffee, and kiwifruit genomes identified a clear pattern of multiplicons (1:5 or 1:6 ratio) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>). Depth analysis of duplicated blocks harboring paralogous genes under the Dc-α fourfold-degenerate transversion peak indicated over-retention of duplicated blocks. In contrast, duplicated blocks harboring paralogous genes under the Dc-β peak retained a larger number of triplicated blocks (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig3">Fig. 3e</a>). We suggest that at least 60 chromosome fusions or translocations and a lineage-specific WGT (Dc-β) followed by a WGD (Dc-α) contributed to diversification of the 9 carrot chromosomes from the 21-chromosome intermediate ancestor.</p><p>Characterization of Dc-α and Dc-β duplicated blocks demonstrated that extensive gene fractionation has occurred during the evolutionary history of the carrot genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 20 and 21</a>). Dc-α ohnologs are significantly enriched (<i>P</i> ≤ 0.01) in protein domains involved in selective molecule interactions (binding) and protein dimerization functions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 22</a>), supporting the gene dosage hypothesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lang, D. et al. Genome-wide phylogenetic comparative analysis of plant transcriptional regulation: a timeline of loss, gain, expansion, and correlation with complexity. Genome Biol. Evol. 2, 488–503 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1843">34</a></sup>; this observation predicts that categories of genes encoding interacting products will likely be over-retained.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Regulatory genes</h3><p>Characterization of orthologous gene clusters across multiple genomes identified 26,320 carrot genes in 13,881 families, with 10,530 genes unique to carrot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). Protein domains involved in regulatory functions (binding) and signaling pathways (protein kinases) were abundant among the genes unique to carrot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 23 and 24</a>).</p><p>We identified 3,267 (10% of the total) regulatory genes in carrot, a number similar to that in tomato (3,209 regulatory genes) and rice (3,203 regulatory genes) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 25 and 26</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>). Overall, genomes that experienced WGDs after the γ paleohexaploidization event harbored more regulatory genes. In carrot, large-scale duplications represented the most common mode of regulatory gene expansion, with <span class="stix">∼</span>33% of these genes retained after the two carrot WGDs, demonstrating the evolutionary impact of large-scale duplications on plant regulatory network diversity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lang, D. et al. Genome-wide phylogenetic comparative analysis of plant transcriptional regulation: a timeline of loss, gain, expansion, and correlation with complexity. Genome Biol. Evol. 2, 488–503 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1871">34</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 27</a>). Six regulatory gene families involved in lineage-specific duplications were expanded in carrot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 28</a>). The expanded families include a zinc-finger (ZF-GFR) regulatory gene family, the JmjC, TCP, and GeBP families, the B3 superfamily, and response regulators. The over-represented regulatory gene subgroups shared orthologous relationships with functionally characterized genes involved in cytokinin signaling, which can influence the circadian clock as well as plant morphology and architecture (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Figs. 16–20</a>). For example, the expanded JmjC, response regulator, and B3-domain subgroups share ancestry with the <i>Arabidopsis thaliana REF6</i>; <i>PRR5</i>, <i>PRR6</i>, and <i>PRR7</i>; and <i>VRN1</i> genes, respectively, which regulate flowering time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Noh, B. et al. Divergent roles of a pair of homologous jumonji/zinc-finger-class transcription factor proteins in the regulation of Arabidopsis flowering time. Plant Cell 16, 2601–2613 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1901">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Nakamichi, N. et al. Arabidopsis clock-associated pseudo-response regulators PRR9, PRR7 and PRR5 coordinately and positively regulate flowering time through the canonical CONSTANS-dependent photoperiodic pathway. Plant Cell Physiol. 48, 822–832 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1904">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Levy, Y.Y., Mesnage, S., Mylne, J.S., Gendall, A.R. &amp; Dean, C. Multiple roles of Arabidopsis VRN1 in vernalization and flowering time control. Science 297, 243–246 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1907">37</a></sup>, a major trait in plant adaption and survival.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Pest and disease resistance genes</h3><p>Using the MATRIX-R pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Sanseverino, W. et al. PRGdb 2.0: towards a community-based database model for the analysis of R-genes in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, D1167–D1171 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1919">38</a></sup> with additional manual data curation, we predicted 634 putative pest and disease resistance (R) genes in carrot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 29–34</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>). Most R gene classes were under-represented in carrot. The expanded orthologous subgroups included classes containing the NBS and LRR protein domains (NL) and coiled-coil NBS and LRR domains (CNL). Lineage-specific duplications contributed to the expansion and diversification of these R gene families in carrot and other genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 21</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 35</a>). Many R genes (206) were located in clusters, and these clusters tended to harbor genes from multiple R gene classes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 36 and 37</a>). The expansion of the NL and CNL families might reflect evolutionary events generating tandem duplications, resulting in preferential clustering on chromosomes 2 and 3–7, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). One cluster containing three RLK genes and one LRR gene, spanning only 50 kb, colocalized with the carrot <i>Mj-1</i> region, which controls resistance to <i>Meloidogyne javanica</i>, a major carrot pest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Simon, P.W., Matthews, W.C. &amp; Roberts, P.A. Evidence for simply inherited dominant resistance to Meloidogyne javanica in carrot. Theor. Appl. Genet. 100, 735–742 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1948">39</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). This analysis demonstrates the important role of tandem duplications in the expansion of R genes in carrot. Additionally, R gene clusters may provide a reservoir of genetic diversity for evolving new plant–pathogen interactions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">A candidate gene controlling high carotenoid accumulation</h3><p>Carotenoids were first discovered in carrot and named accordingly. The <i>Y</i> and <i>Y</i><sub>2</sub> gene model explains the phenotypic differences between white and orange carrots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Buishand, J.G. &amp; Gabelman, W.H. Investigations on the inheritance of color and carotenoid content in phloem and xylem of carrot roots (Daucus carota L.). Euphytica 28, 611–632 (1979)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1970">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Just, B.J., Santos, C.A., Yandell, B.S. &amp; Simon, P.W. Major QTL for carrot color are positionally associated with carotenoid biosynthetic genes and interact epistatically in a domesticated × wild carrot cross. Theor. Appl. Genet. 119, 1155–1169 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1973">41</a></sup>, with elevated carotenoid accumulation in homozygous-recessive genotypes (<i>yyy</i><sub>2</sub><i>y</i><sub>2</sub>). In spite of the striking color variation attributed to these two genes, little is known about the molecular basis of carotenoid accumulation in carrot. Although homologs of all known carotenoid biosynthesis genes have been identified in carrot, none appear to be responsible for carotenoid accumulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Just, B.J. et al. Carotenoid biosynthesis structural genes in carrot (Daucus carota): isolation, sequence-characterization, single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers and genome mapping. Theor. Appl. Genet. 114, 693–704 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1985">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Clotault, J. et al. Expression of carotenoid biosynthesis genes during carrot root development. J. Exp. Bot. 59, 3563–3573 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1988">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Fuentes, P. et al. Light-dependent changes in plastid differentiation influence carotenoid gene expression and accumulation in carrot roots. Plant Mol. Biol. 79, 47–59 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1991">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bowman, M.J., Willis, D.K. &amp; Simon, P.W. Transcript abundance of phytoene synthase 1 and phytoene synthase 2 is associated with natural variation of storage root carotenoid pigmentation in carrot. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 139, 63–68 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1994">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Wang, H., Ou, C.-G., Zhuang, F.-Y. &amp; Ma, Z.-G. The dual role of phytoene synthase genes in carotenogenesis in carrot roots and leaves. Mol. Breed. 34, 2065–2079 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d2836e1997">46</a></sup>. Using two mapping populations, we demonstrated that <i>Y</i> regulates high carotenoid accumulation in both yellow and dark orange roots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Figs. 23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 38</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>), a result consistent with the previously proposed model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Just, B.J., Santos, C.A., Yandell, B.S. &amp; Simon, P.W. Major QTL for carrot color are positionally associated with carotenoid biosynthetic genes and interact epistatically in a domesticated × wild carrot cross. Theor. Appl. Genet. 119, 1155–1169 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2020">41</a></sup>. Fine-mapping analysis identified a 75-kb region on chromosome 5 that harbors the <i>Y</i> gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig4">Fig. 4b–e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 25</a>). Of the eight genes predicted in this region, none had homology with known isoprenoid biosynthesis genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 39</a>), implying that regulation of carotenoid accumulation in carrot roots by the <i>Y</i> locus extends beyond the isoprenoid biosynthesis genes. Within the 75-kb region, DCAR_032551 was the only gene to have a mutation that segregated with high carotenoid pigmentation. DCAR_032551 harbors a 212-nt insertion in its second exon that creates a frameshift mutation in both yellow and dark orange carrots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 26</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 39</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Phenotypes, candidate genes, and transcriptome changes associated with carotenoid accumulation in carrot roots."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Phenotypes, candidate genes, and transcriptome changes associated with carotenoid accumulation in carrot roots.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1012"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Phenotypes associated with the <i>Y</i> locus, including pale orange (pOr), dark orange (dOr), yellow (Y), and white (W) roots, from the indicated populations. (<b>b</b>) Previously published genetic map and location of the <i>Y</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Just, B.J., Santos, C.A., Yandell, B.S. &amp; Simon, P.W. Major QTL for carrot color are positionally associated with carotenoid biosynthetic genes and interact epistatically in a domesticated × wild carrot cross. Theor. Appl. Genet. 119, 1155–1169 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2070">41</a></sup>. (<b>c</b>) Carrot chromosome 5 and the molecular markers used for fine-mapping of the <i>Y</i> locus. The genotypes of the 76-kb region in recombinant individuals are illustrated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 25</a>). Het, heterozygous. (<b>d</b>) The fine-mapped region controlling the <i>Y</i> locus. Numbers represent the eight genes predicted in this region. Gene 7, DCAR_032551, was the only gene differentially expressed (upregulated) in RNA-seq analysis of yellow versus white and dark orange versus pale orange samples. Below are all the nonsynonymous SNPs (for example, G&gt;A) and insertions (ins) identified in the four genes located in the 65-kb haplotype block associated with the <i>Y</i> locus in the resequencing samples (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 40</a>). The number of accessions with each haplotype block classification (I–III; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 17</a>) is given. The DCAR_032551 <i>y</i><sup><i>a</i></sup> variant harbors a 212-nt insertion in the second exon, and the <i>y</i><sup><i>b</i></sup> variant harbors a 1-nt insertion in the second exon. Het, heterozygous. (<b>e</b>,<b>f</b>) Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) estimated in wild (blue) and cultivated (orange) carrots (<b>e</b>) and the top 1% of <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values (blue) (<b>f</b>) in the 75-kb region (gray shading) of carrot chromosome 5. (<b>g</b>,<b>h</b>) Patterns of LD in wild (<b>g</b>) and cultivated (<b>h</b>) carrots. Red and white spots indicate regions of strong (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 1) and weak (<i>r</i><sup>2</sup> = 0) LD, respectively. The gray bar indicates the position of the 75-kb fine-mapped region harboring the <i>Y</i> candidate gene.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using resequencing data, a haplotype block extending for 65 kb, with 64 kb overlapping the fine-mapped region, was associated with all but two highly pigmented root samples (C1 and I2) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 27</a>). In contrast, within the 65-kb region, seven haplotype blocks were detected in wild accessions. Polymorphism detection within the haplotype block identified eight nonsynonymous SNPs in four genes and two indels, including the 212-nt insertion in DCAR_032551, in yellow and dark orange samples (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig4">Fig. 4f</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 40</a>). No wild or cultivated white samples had the 212-nt insertion. The two highly pigmented (<i>yy</i>) accessions, C1 and I2, that did not share the 65-kb haplotype block were heterozygous for the insertion. However, further analysis of DCAR_032551 identified a 1-nt insertion in the second exon, 60 nt upstream of the 212-nt insertion site (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig4">Fig. 4f</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 26</a>). The 1-nt insertion was in <i>trans</i> phase relative to the 212-nt insertion, indicating that these accessions harbor two frameshift mutations that likely disrupt functioning of the <i>Y</i> gene product. Thus, resequencing supports the central role of DCAR_032551 in conditioning high pigment accumulation in carrot roots and identifies a second, independent mutation in this same gene, which we speculate should also be recessive to the wild-type allele.</p><p>To determine whether this region was ever under selection, we scanned for differences in nucleotide diversity, differentiation, and linkage disequilibrium (LD) between wild and cultivated accessions. An <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> peak on chromosome 5, located between 24.4 and 25.0 Mb, overlapped the 75-kb fine-mapped region underlying DCAR_032551 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig2">Figs. 2c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig4">4g,h</a>). In this region, LD was increased in highly pigmented cultivated materials and nucleotide diversity was drastically reduced in cultivated carrots (wild, <i>π</i> = 3.1 × 10<sup>−4</sup> versus cultivated, <i>π</i> = 2.0 × 10<sup>−4</sup>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig4">Fig. 4g,h</a>). The 50-kb window encompassing the <i>Y</i> candidate gene had the highest level of differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> = 1.0) and the lowest level of nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i> = 1.5 × 10<sup>−4</sup>) among cultivated carrots. The selective sweep in the <i>Y</i> region is relatively short in comparison with those for other genes controlling carotenoid accumulation, including the selective sweep for <i>y1</i> in maize, which extends 200 kb upstream and 600 kb downstream of the gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Palaisa, K., Morgante, M., Tingey, S. &amp; Rafalski, A. Long-range patterns of diversity and linkage disequilibrium surrounding the maize Y1 gene are indicative of an asymmetric selective sweep. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9885–9890 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2239">47</a></sup>. Rather, this scenario resembles the short sweep (60–90 kb) identified in maize around <i>teosinte branched1</i> (<i>tb1</i>), a major domestication-associated gene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Wang, R.-L., Stec, A., Hey, J., Lukens, L. &amp; Doebley, J. The limits of selection during maize domestication. Nature 398, 236–239 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2250">48</a></sup>. A short sweep may reflect the highly effective rates of recombination expected in an outcrossing species like carrot. Gene flow between wild and cultivated carrot followed by recurrent phenotypic selection that likely occurred throughout the history of carrot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Simon, P.W. Domestication, historical development, and modern breeding of carrot. Plant Breed. Rev. 19, 157–190 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2254">4</a></sup> may have had a role in increasing the recombination rate around the <i>Y</i> locus.</p><p>Selection signatures, including reduction in nucleotide diversity and a decrease in the number of haplotypes, associated with the <i>Y</i> gene region further support the inclusion of carotenoid accumulation as a major domestication trait—a trait that contributes substantial nutritional and economic value to modern carrots. Furthermore, the identification of a second haplotype block for pigmentation surrounding the <i>Y</i> candidate gene suggests that this gene has been selected multiple times. These results may elucidate the timing and origin of the pigmented taproot phenotype during carrot domestication.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">A model for carotenoid accumulation in carrot roots</h3><p>To investigate gene expression in the region of the <i>Y</i> candidate, comparative transcriptome analysis was performed for white versus yellow and pale orange versus dark orange roots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>). DCAR_032551 was the only significantly differentially expressed (upregulated; <i>P</i> ≤ 0.001) gene in the <i>yy</i> (yellow and dark orange) relative to the <i>Y</i>– (white and pale orange) genotype (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 39</a>), further supporting our mapping and resequencing results.</p><p>Weighted gene coexpression network analysis (WGCNA) indicated that DCAR_032551 is coordinated with a set of 925 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 41</a>).
