Gene Ontology (GO) term enrichment analysis indicated that isoprenoid pathway genes were particularly enriched (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 42</a>). Among cellular components, membrane terms and molecular function terms related to oxidative reactions and biological processes in response to acids and chemicals were highly enriched (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 43</a>). Assuming a conserved function of <i>Y</i> in yellow and dark orange roots, we annotated genes that were differentially expressed (upregulated or downregulated) in white versus yellow and pale orange versus dark orange comparisons. This analysis identified a positive relationship between high carotenoid accumulation and overexpression of several light-induced genes, including those involved in photosynthetic system activation and function, plastid biogenesis, and chlorophyll metabolism (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 44 and 45</a>), an unexpected finding in non-photosynthetic root tissue. These findings tie into the WGCNA analysis as components of photomorphogenesis are located in the thylakoid membranes and involve many oxidative processes and chemical responses, including hormonal regulation. Analysis of the 98 genes annotated in the plastidal methylerythritol phosphate (MEP) and carotenoid pathways (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 46</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>) confirmed coordinated overexpression of several genes in these pathways and carotenoid accumulation in <i>yy</i> plants. Furthermore, an inverse relationship was observed between the majority of differentially expressed terpene synthase genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 47</a>) and high carotenoid accumulation, consistent with substrate flux into the carotenoid pathway. <i>DXS1</i> and <i>LCYE</i> were the only genes in the MEP and carotenoid pathways that were differentially expressed in <i>yy</i> genotype samples with high carotenoid accumulation in both populations, suggesting that they possibly encode enzymes that regulate carotenoid accumulation. Although <i>LCYE</i> has not been reported to be a carotenoid regulatory gene target, its elevated expression may account for the relative abundance of lutein in yellow carrots and alpha-carotene in orange carrots. DXS1 is a limiting factor in upregulation of the carotenoid pathway in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ruiz-Sola, M.Á. &amp; Rodríguez-Concepción, M. Carotenoid biosynthesis in Arabidopsis: a colorful pathway. Arabidopsis Book 10, e0158 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2343">49</a></sup>. DXS1 expression is induced by light<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Cordoba, E. et al. Functional characterization of the three genes encoding 1-deoxy-D-xylulose 5-phosphate synthase in maize. J. Exp. Bot. 62, 2023–2038 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2347">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kim, B.R., Kim, S.U. &amp; Chang, Y.J. Differential expression of three 1-deoxy-D-xylulose-5-phosphate synthase genes in rice. Biotechnol. Lett. 27, 997–1001 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2350">51</a></sup>, and it is the main DXS isoform catalyzing the biosynthesis of isoprenoid and carotenoid precursors in photosynthetic metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Saladié, M., Wright, L.P., Garcia-Mas, J., Rodriguez-Concepcion, M. &amp; Phillips, M.A. The 2-C-methylerythritol 4-phosphate pathway in melon is regulated by specialized isoforms for the first and last steps. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 5077–5092 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2354">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Walter, M.H., Hans, J. &amp; Strack, D. Two distantly related genes encoding 1-deoxy-D-xylulose 5-phosphate synthases: differential regulation in shoots and apocarotenoid-accumulating mycorrhizal roots. Plant J. 31, 243–254 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2357">53</a></sup>. DXS1 also regulates carotenoid accumulation in <i>A. thaliana</i> and tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Estévez, J.M., Cantero, A., Reindl, A., Reichler, S. &amp; León, P. 1-Deoxy-D-xylulose-5-phosphate synthase, a limiting enzyme for plastidic isoprenoid biosynthesis in plants. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 22901–22909 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2365">54</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lois, L.M., Rodríguez-Concepción, M., Gallego, F., Campos, N. &amp; Boronat, A. Carotenoid biosynthesis during tomato fruit development: regulatory role of 1-deoxy-D-xylulose 5-phosphate synthase. Plant J. 22, 503–513 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2368">55</a></sup>. Overall, these results indicate that DCAR_032551 is coexpressed with isoprenoid pathway genes and that overexpression of the light-induced/photosynthetic transcriptome cascades in orange and yellow carrot roots may explain elevated carotenoid accumulation.</p><p>The DCAR_032551 gene product represents a plant-specific protein of unknown function, and mutants of the <i>A. thaliana</i> homolog PSEUDO-ETIOLATION IN LIGHT (PEL) have an etiolated phenotype, a phenotype associated with defective responses to light<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Ichikawa, T. et al. The FOX hunting system: an alternative gain-of-function gene hunting technique. Plant J. 48, 974–985 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2378">56</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 44</a>). In many ways, the physiology and genetics of carotenoid accumulation in dark orange and yellow (<i>yy</i>) carrots are similar to the phenotypes of the <i>A. thaliana det</i>, <i>cop</i>, and <i>fus</i> de-etiolated mutants. These mutants lack the ability to inhibit the light-induced photosynthetic transcriptome cascade associated with de-etiolation and photomorphogenesis in non-photosynthetic tissues such as roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Huang, X., Ouyang, X. &amp; Deng, X.W. Beyond repression of photomorphogenesis: role switching of COP/DET/FUS in light signaling. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 21, 96–103 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2398">57</a></sup>. De-etiolated mutants grown in the dark have characteristics of light-grown seedlings, including carotenoid accumulation and overexpression of light-induced photosystem and plastid biogenesis genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wei, N. &amp; Deng, X.-W. The role of the COP/DET/FUS genes in light control of Arabidopsis seedling development. Plant Physiol. 112, 871–878 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2402">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lau, O.S. &amp; Deng, X.W. The photomorphogenic repressors COP1 and DET1: 20 years later. Trends Plant Sci. 17, 584–593 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2405">59</a></sup>. In contrast, when exposed to light, these mutants demonstrate ectopic expression of genes involved in chloroplast formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wei, N. &amp; Deng, X.-W. The role of the COP/DET/FUS genes in light control of Arabidopsis seedling development. Plant Physiol. 112, 871–878 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2409">58</a></sup>. Physiological studies have demonstrated that, unlike other species, carrots with carotenoid-rich roots have ectopic chloroplast accumulation when exposed to light<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Fuentes, P. et al. Light-dependent changes in plastid differentiation influence carotenoid gene expression and accumulation in carrot roots. Plant Mol. Biol. 79, 47–59 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2413">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Rodriguez-Concepcion, M. &amp; Stange, C. Biosynthesis of carotenoids in carrot: an underground story comes to light. Arch. Biochem. Biophys. 539, 110–116 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2416">60</a></sup> and that highly pigmented carrot roots have upregulation of photosystem-related genes in comparison with white roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Arbizu, C., Reitsma, K.R., Simon, P.W. &amp; Spooner, D.M. Morphometrics of Daucus (Apiaceae): a counterpart to a phylogenomic study. Am. J. Bot. 101, 2005–2016 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2421">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Bowman, M.J. Gene Expression and Genetic Analysis of Carotenoid Pigment Accumulation in Carrot (Daucus carota L.). PhD thesis, Univ. Wisconsin–Madison, (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2424">61</a></sup>. These observations when coupled with the transcriptome data presented here indicate that, similar to de-etiolated <i>A. thaliana</i> mutants, carrot roots with high levels of carotenoid accumulation may have lost the ability to inhibit the transcriptome cascade associated with de-etiolation and photomorphogenesis. The recessive nature of the <i>Y</i> gene in such roots is compatible with loss of the constitutive negative feedback function associated with the recessive <i>det</i>, <i>cop</i>, and <i>fus</i> mutants in <i>A. thaliana</i>. In addition, the <i>A. thaliana</i> homolog of the <i>Y</i> candidate produces a protein that interacts with genes such as <i>FAR1</i> and <i>COP9</i>, involved in the light signaling pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 48</a>). Our hypothesis is further supported by the WGCNA analysis indicating that DCAR_032551 is coexpressed with <i>COP1</i> and <i>HY5</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 41</a>), genes both directly involved in the regulation of photomorphogenesis. Together, these findings make DCAR_032551 a plausible regulatory candidate. Considering our results coupled with previous physiological studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Fuentes, P. et al. Light-dependent changes in plastid differentiation influence carotenoid gene expression and accumulation in carrot roots. Plant Mol. Biol. 79, 47–59 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2472">44</a></sup>, we hypothesize that carotenoid accumulation in carrot taproot results from root de-etiolation, whereby the repression of photomorphogenic development typically found in etiolated roots is lifted. The resulting overexpression of <i>DXS1</i> provides precursors to the carotenoid biosynthetic pathway, which leads to an accumulation of carotenoids in orange and yellow (<i>yy</i>) carrot roots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Working model of the regulation of carotenoid accumulation in carrot root."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Working model of the regulation of carotenoid accumulation in carrot root.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3565/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3565_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="501"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Upward- and downward-pointing arrows indicate upregulated and downregulated genes, respectively, in the yellow versus white (yellow arrows) and dark orange versus pale orange (orange arrows) comparisons. The orange box delimits the isoprenoid biosynthetic branch that leads to the carotenoid pathway. As shown in the green box, the majority of the upregulated genes in yellow and dark orange roots are involved in the photosynthetic pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 45</a>); genes that are included are involved in the assembly and function of photosystems I and II and plastid development. We hypothesize that loss of the constitutive repression mechanisms conditioned by genes involved in de-etiolation and photomorphogenensis in non-photosynthetic tissue, such as carrot roots, induces overexpression of <i>DXS1</i> and, consequently, activation of the metabolic cascade that leads to high levels of carotenoid accumulation in carrot roots.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3565/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Vitamin A deficiency is a global health challenge<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Sherwin, J.C., Reacher, M.H., Dean, W.H. &amp; Ngondi, J. Epidemiology of vitamin A deficiency and xerophthalmia in at-risk populations. Trans. R. Soc. Trop. Med. Hyg. 106, 205–214 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2521">62</a></sup>, making the development of sustainable vitamin A sources a priority for crop improvement. Its plentiful carotenoids make carrot an important source of provitamin A in the human diet<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Simon, P.W. Plant breeding for human nutritional quality. Plant Breed. Rev. 31, 325–392 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2525">6</a></sup>. Although carrot was a model organism to study plant development and totipotency in the 1950s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Steward, F.C. Growth and organized development of cultured cells. III. Interpretation of the growth from free cell to carrot plant. Am. J. Bot. 45, 709–713 (1958)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2529">63</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Vogel, G. How does a single somatic cell become a whole plant? Science 309, 86 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2532">64</a></sup>, the molecular basis of neither carrot growth nor phytochemical accumulation has been well described. The high-quality carrot genome sequence described here, in combination with mapping and comparative transcriptome analysis, demonstrates that carotenoid accumulation in carrot is controlled at the regulatory level and that root de-etiolation leading to overexpression of the photosynthetic transcriptome cascade may have an important role in this regulatory mechanism. These results provide the foundation for new genetic mechanisms regulating carotene accumulation in plants, with potential application to several crops.</p><p>This study included the first comparative genomic and phylogenomic analyses comprising members of the euasterid II clade and clarified the evolutionary events surrounding the radiation of the main asterid clades. The two new WGD events (Dc-α and Dc-β) identified provide a new tool to study genome polyploidization. The two WGDs specific to the carrot lineage and the new WGD identified in the horseweed genome, which is possibly shared with lettuce, prompt important evolutionary questions about the possible involvement of the latter WGD in the early radiation of the Asterales order. The carrot genome is the first chromosome-scale Apiaceae genome to be sequenced and will provide a foundation for future comparative genomic and evolutionary studies.</p><p>Resequencing diverse <i>Daucus</i> species emphasized a high level of variability in repetitive sequence structure and chromosomal location, demonstrated a high level of genetic diversity retained in cultivated carrots, and identified a genetic sweep associated with domestication. This information lays the groundwork for future studies on carrot domestication and chromosome evolution across the <i>Daucus</i> genus.</p><p>The high-quality carrot reference genome and large set of SNP markers will accelerate marker-facilitated trait mapping through genome-wide association studies and genomic selection. The carrot genome sequence will support crop improvement efforts and help identify additional candidate genes underlying isoprenoid and flavonoid accumulation, biotic and abiotic stress resistance, and regulatory pathways controlling growth, flowering, seed production, and regeneration in tissue culture—all important traits for sustained agricultural production and improved human health.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Plant materials and sequencing.</h3><p>Genome assembly used doubled-haploid NCBI BioSample <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN03216637">SAMN03216637</a>. Sequences included 3 paired-end Illumina libraries, 5 mate-paired Illumina libraries, and 40,693 BAC end sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 49 and 50</a>). The abundance of 17-nt <i>k</i>-mers from 170- and 800-nt libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 28</a>) was used to estimate the genome size (k_num/peak depth) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14"><i>De novo</i> assembly.</h3><p>Genome assembly used SOAPdenovo version 2.04 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2592">67</a>). Gaps were filled using GapCloser. This generated assembly v1.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 51</a>).</p><p>To guide the construction of superscaffolds and anchor the genome, an integrated linkage map was developed using JoinMap 4.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Van Ooijen, J.W. JoinMap 4: Software for the Calculation of Genetic Linkage Maps in Experimental Populations (Kyazma, 2006)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2601">68</a>). CheckMatrix (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>) was used to remove markers with inconsistent placement. The collinearity of common markers was inspected using MapChart 2.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Voorrips, R.E. MapChart: software for the graphical presentation of linkage maps and QTLs. J. Hered. 93, 77–78 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2607">69</a>), and inconsistent markers were removed before merging maps. Markers in common were used as anchor points (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 52 and 53</a>). Marker order correlations between composite and component map linkage groups were calculated in SAS 9.2 using the PROC CORR Spearman function (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 54</a>). Linkage groups were assigned to chromosomes, oriented, and numbered using published classification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Iovene, M. et al. Comparative FISH mapping of Daucus species (Apiaceae family). Chromosome Res. 19, 493–506 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2618">23</a></sup>.</p><p>To build superscaffolds and to identify chimeric scaffolds and correct them, 29,875 paired-end BACs, 20-kb and 40-kb Illumina mate-paired sequences, and 2,075 marker sequences mapped in the carrot integrated linkage map were aligned to the v1.0 assembly. For each scaffold or contig, unambiguously aligned sequences were visualized in GBrowse. Superscaffolding was initiated with scaffolds containing sequences of mapped markers. Scaffold connections supported by at least two paired-end BACs were annotated, and sequences were further connected using a custom Perl script (cp1; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>). The quality of each scaffold assembly and contiguity were verified by visually inspecting the coverage of large-insert libraries (20 and 40 kb) and the consistency of marker order along the linkage map.</p><p>Possible chimeric scaffolds (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM296">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>) were identified as those containing sequences of markers mapped to different linkage groups or to distal locations of the same linkage group or those containing regions not covered by mate-paired sequences. Within each chimeric scaffold, the chimeric regions were identified as those regions not covered by mate-paired or paired-end BAC sequences and were then manually inspected. The midpoint between the closest unambiguously aligned paired-end sequences flanking the chimeric region was defined as the misassembly point. Corrected scaffolds were then used to progressively construct superscaffolds as described above. This process generated assembly v2.0 and nine carrot pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Figs. 29</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">30</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 55</a>).</p><p>See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a> for additional details.</p><p><i>De novo</i> assembly of the plastid and mitochondrial genomes is described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome quality evaluation.</h3><p>The presence of possible sequence contamination was evaluated using DeconSeq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Schmieder, R. &amp; Edwards, R. Fast identification and removal of sequence contamination from genomic and metagenomic datasets. PLoS One 6, e17288 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2666">70</a></sup> with scaftigs from the v2.0 assembly.</p><p>To evaluate the correctness of the assembled sequences, we used (i) an 8-kb 454 library of DH1 (SRA accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX1135252">SRX1135252</a>) and (ii) 4,717 paired-end BACs that were not used to join scaffolds into superscaffolds during assembly. Paired-end reads that aligned with both ends to a unique location in the carrot plastid genome or the v2.0 assembly were used to calculate the mean insert size.</p><p>A new linkage map including GBS SNP markers was developed to verify the order of the scaffolds and superscaffolds. GBS libraries were prepared as described by Elshire <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Elshire, R.J. et al. A robust, simple genotyping-by-sequencing (GBS) approach for high diversity species. PLoS One 6, e19379 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2686">71</a></sup>, with minimal modification. TASSEL version 4.3.11 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Bradbury, P.J. et al. TASSEL: software for association mapping of complex traits in diverse samples. Bioinformatics 23, 2633–2635 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2689">72</a>) was used for analysis, with paired-end data preprocessed for TASSEL compatibility using a custom Perl script, bb.tassel (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>). SNPs were called using documented GBS pipeline procedures<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Glaubitz, J.C. et al. TASSEL-GBS: a high capacity genotyping by sequencing analysis pipeline. PLoS One 9, e90346 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2696">73</a></sup>. Sequences containing SNPs unambiguously aligned to the carrot genome assembly were kept (18,007 SNPs). SNPs scored as heterozygous but with an allele ratio a:b far from 1:1 were eliminated if the ratio was &lt;0.3 or &gt;3.0, where a and b were the two alleles for a given SNP. Mapping was carried out as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Cavagnaro, P.F. et al. A gene-derived SNP-based high resolution linkage map of carrot including the location of QTL conditioning root and leaf anthocyanin pigmentation. BMC Genomics 15, 1118 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2701">74</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 31</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>FISH experiments were carried out to evaluate consistency and coverage of the carrot genome assembly in telomeric regions. Anther preparation and the FISH procedure were carried out according to published protocols<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Dong, F. et al. Development and applications of a set of chromosome-specific cytogenetic DNA markers in potato. Theor. Appl. Genet. 101, 1001–1007 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2714">75</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Iovene, M., Grzebelus, E., Carputo, D., Jiang, J. &amp; Simon, P.W. Major cytogenetic landmarks and karyotype analysis inDaucus carota and other Apiaceae. Am. J. Bot. 95, 793–804 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2717">76</a></sup> using five types of probes: (i) BAC probes specific for subtelomeric regions on the short (1S, 2S, 4S, 5S, 6S, 8S, 9S) and long (1L, 2L, 4L, 5L, 6L, 8L, 9L) arms of each chromosome, (ii) carrot chromosome-specific BAC probes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Iovene, M. et al. Comparative FISH mapping of Daucus species (Apiaceae family). Chromosome Res. 19, 493–506 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2721">23</a></sup>, (iii) telomeric probe (Telo), (iv) a probe corresponding to the CL80 repetitive sequence, and (v) plasmid K11 containing the putative carrot centromere repeat (Cent-Dc)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Iovene, M. et al. Comparative FISH mapping of Daucus species (Apiaceae family). Chromosome Res. 19, 493–506 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2725">23</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 56</a>).</p><p>Gene space coverage was evaluated using carrot ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the carrot transcriptome reveals novel genes, new markers, and genetic diversity. BMC Genomics 12, 389 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2736">14</a></sup>, RNA-seq data from 20 different DH1 tissues (NCBI BioSamples <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN03965304">SAMN03965304</a>–<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN03965323">SAMN03965323</a>), and 258 ultraconserved genes from the Core Eukaryotic Genes data set. Previously published carrot ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the carrot transcriptome reveals novel genes, new markers, and genetic diversity. BMC Genomics 12, 389 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2754">14</a></sup> were aligned to the genome using BLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Altschul, S.F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E.W. &amp; Lipman, D.J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2758">77</a></sup>; RNA-seq data from 20 different DH1 tissues (NCBI BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA291977">PRJNA291977</a>) were assembled with Trinity r2013_08_14 and mapped to the assembly using TopHat v2.0.11 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2769">78</a>). Scaftigs from the carrot assembly were aligned to the Core Eukaryotic Genes data set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2773">15</a></sup> using CEGMA v2.4.</p><p>See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a> for additional details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Repetitive sequences, gene prediction, and genome annotation.</h3><p>RepeatMasker v3.2.9 (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>) was applied to screen the genome assembly for low-complexity DNA sequences and interspersed repeated elements using a custom library. <i>Ab initio</i> prediction with RepeatModeler version 1.1.0.4 (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>) generated a <i>de novo</i> repeat library from the assembled genome. RepeatMasker and LTR_FINDER version 1.1.0.5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2802">79</a>) were then used to identify and classify repeat elements in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>MITEs belonging to the <i>Tourist</i>-like <i>Krak</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Grzebelus, D., Yau, Y.-Y. &amp; Simon, P.W. Master: A novel family of PIF/Harbinger-like transposable elements identified in carrot (Daucus carota L.). Mol. Genet. Genomics 275, 450–459 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2821">18</a></sup> and <i>Stowaway</i>-like <i>DcSto</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Macko-Podgorni, A., Nowicka, A., Grzebelus, E., Simon, P.W. &amp; Grzebelus, D. DcSto: carrot Stowaway-like elements are abundant, diverse, and polymorphic. Genetica 141, 255–267 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2830">19</a></sup> families were identified using TIRfinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Gambin, T. et al. TIRfinder: a web tool for mining class II transposons carrying terminal inverted repeats. Evol. Bioinform. Online 9, 17–27 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2835">80</a></sup>, including the carrot, kiwifruit, pepper, tomato, and potato genomes. MITE copies were grouped into families fulfilling the 80–80–80 criterion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973–982 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2839">81</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 33</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 57–59</a>). Consensus sequences were used to investigate intra- and interspecific relationships among families with Circoletto<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Darzentas, N. Circoletto: visualizing sequence similarity with Circos. Bioinformatics 26, 2620–2621 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2849">82</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2852">83</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 34</a>). <i>Stowaway</i>-like elements carrying insertions &gt;10 nt in length were removed from subsequent steps. Within-family similarity was calculated from a Kimura two-parameter pairwise distance matrix. The evolutionary history of related <i>DcSto</i> elements was investigated using MEGA6 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725–2729 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2865">84</a>).</p><p>Tandem repetitive sequences were analyzed with RepeatExplorer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Novák, P., Neumann, P., Pech, J., Steinhaisl, J. &amp; Macas, J. RepeatExplorer: a Galaxy-based web server for genome-wide characterization of eukaryotic repetitive elements from next-generation sequence reads. Bioinformatics 29, 792–793 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2872">22</a></sup> and SeqGrapheR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Novák, P., Neumann, P. &amp; Macas, J. Graph-based clustering and characterization of repetitive sequences in next-generation sequencing data. BMC Bioinformatics 11, 378 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2876">85</a></sup> using a subset of 1 × 10<sup>7</sup> Illumina reads from DH1 and five resequenced genotypes representative of <i>Daucus</i> clades I and II. To select tandem repetitive sequences, the node/edge ratio (number of nodes/number of edges) among aligned sequences in each cluster was calculated. Clusters with a ratio &gt;0.09, representing more than 0.05% of the genome, were selected for further analysis. Tandem repeats were identified using Tandem Repeats Finder v4.07b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2885">86</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 60 and 61</a>).</p><p>The abundance and localization of selected repetitive sequences in DH1 and other <i>Daucus</i> species were also investigated by FISH (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>For gene model prediction, mobile element–related repeats were masked using RepeatMasker (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>). <i>De novo</i> prediction using AUGUSTUS v2.5.5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2911">87</a>), GENSCAN v.1.1.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78–94 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2914">88</a>), and GlimmerHMM-3.0.1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Majoros, W.H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2917">89</a>) was trained using model species <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>S. lycoperisum</i> training sets. The protein sequences of <i>S. lycoperisum</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>Brassica rapa</i>, and <i>Oryza sativa</i> were mapped to the carrot genome using TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Altschul, S.F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E.W. &amp; Lipman, D.J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2944">77</a></sup> (BLAST All 2.2.23) and analyzed with GeneWise version 2.2.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2947">90</a>). Carrot ESTs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the carrot transcriptome reveals novel genes, new markers, and genetic diversity. BMC Genomics 12, 389 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2951">14</a></sup> were aligned to the genome using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Kent, W.J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2955">91</a></sup> and analyzed with PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Campbell, M.A., Haas, B.J., Hamilton, J.P., Mount, S.M. &amp; Buell, C.R. Comprehensive analysis of alternative splicing in rice and comparative analyses with Arabidopsis. BMC Genomics 7, 327 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2959">92</a></sup> to detect spliced gene models. RNA-seq reads from 20 DH1 libraries were aligned with TopHat 2.0.9 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2963">78</a>). Transcripts were predicted by Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2967">93</a></sup>. All gene models produced by <i>de novo</i> prediction, protein homology searches, and prediction and transcript-based evidence were integrated using GLEAN v1.1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2973">94</a>).</p><p>Putative gene functions were assigned using the best BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Altschul, S.F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E.W. &amp; Lipman, D.J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2980">77</a></sup> match to SwissProt and TrEMBL databases. Gene motifs and domains were determined with InterProScan version 4.7 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Zdobnov, E.M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan—an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2983">95</a>) against the ProDom, PRINTS, Pfam, SMART, PANTHER, and PROSITE protein databases. GO IDs for each gene were obtained from the corresponding InterPro entries. All genes were aligned against KEGG (release 58) proteins.</p><p>miRNAs and snRNAs in the assembled genome were detected using INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Nawrocki, E.P. &amp; Eddy, S.R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933–2935 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2990">96</a></sup> against the Rfam database (release 9.1). tRNA loci were detected using tRNAscan-SE v1.1.23 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Lowe, T.M. &amp; Eddy, S.R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2993">97</a>). rRNA was detected by homologous BLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Altschul, S.F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E.W. &amp; Lipman, D.J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d2836e2997">77</a></sup> searches using the closest available species with complete sequences, <i>Panax ginseng</i>, <i>P. quinquefolius</i>, and <i>Thapsia garganica</i> (accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=KM036295.1">KM036295.1</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=KM036296.1">KM036296.1</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=KM036297.1">KM036297.1</a>, and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AJ007917.1">AJ007917.1</a>).</p><p>See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a> for additional details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Resequencing.</h3><p>Resequencing data under NCBI BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA291976">PRJNA291976</a> (BioSamples <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN03766317">SAMN03766317</a>–<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN03766351">SAMN03766351</a>) include 18 cultivated accessions, 13 wild accessions, and 4 other <i>Daucus</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 16</a>).</p><p>DNA from single plants was extracted as described by Murray and Thompson<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Murray, M.G. &amp; Thompson, W.F. Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 8, 4321–4325 (1980)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3084">98</a></sup>. Paired-end libraries with insert sizes of 250–350 nt were sequenced using Illumina technology at BGI.</p><p>Reads were mapped with BWA-MEM version 0.7.10 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3090">99</a>). Alignments were filtered using SAMtools version 0.1.19 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3093">100</a>). Duplicate reads were marked using MarkDuplicates from Picard tools version 1.119 (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>). GATK version 3.3-0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3099">101</a>) was used to identify SNPs for each genotype.</p><p>The accuracy of SNP calls was evaluated with 3,202 previously characterized SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. Genetic structure and domestication of carrot (Daucus carota subsp. sativus) (Apiaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 930–938 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3106">3</a></sup>. A random subset of 49,365 biallelic SNPs was analyzed with STRUCTURE v2.3.4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Pritchard, J.K., Stephens, M. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945–959 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3109">102</a>), and the most accurate population structure was determined by the method discussed in Evanno <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Evanno, G., Regnaut, S. &amp; Goudet, J. Detecting the number of clusters of individuals using the software STRUCTURE: a simulation study. Mol. Ecol. 14, 2611–2620 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3116">103</a></sup>.</p><p>Phylogenic analysis used PHYLIP v3.5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Felsenstein, J. PHYLIP—Phylogeny Inference Package (Version 3.2). Cladistics 5, 164–166 (1989)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3123">104</a>) with this same subset. Seqboot was used for bootstrapping with 1,000 replicates, and genetic distances were calculated using gendist. A neighbor-joining tree was created using the neighbor function, and a consensus tree was generated using consense.</p><p>See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a> for additional details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome evolution.</h3><p>Gene clusters with 13 other species were identified using OrthoMCL v2.0.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3140">105</a>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 19 and 62</a>).</p><p>Peptide sequence from 312 single-copy orthologous gene clusters was used to construct phylogenetic relationships and estimate divergence time. Alignments from MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3150">106</a></sup> were converted to coding sequences. Fourfold-degenerate sites were concatenated and used to estimate the neutral substitution rate per year and divergence time. PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307–321 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3154">107</a></sup> was used to construct the phylogenetic tree.</p><p>The Bayesian Relaxed Molecular Clock (BRMC) approach was used to estimate the species divergence time using the program MCMCTREE v4.0, which is part of the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3161">108</a></sup>. The 'correlated molecular clock' and 'JC69' models were used. Published times for sorghum–rice (&lt;55 million years ago, &gt;35 million years ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 549–554 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3165">109</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Paterson, A.H., Bowers, J.E. &amp; Chapman, B.A. Ancient polyploidization predating divergence of the cereals, and its consequences for comparative genomics. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 101, 9903–9908 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3168">110</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3171">111</a></sup>, tomato–potato (&lt;4 million years ago, &gt;2 million years ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3175">112</a></sup>, and grape–rice (&lt;130 million years ago, &gt;240 million years ago)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3179">113</a></sup> divergence were used to calibrate divergence time.</p><p>Chromosome collinearity within carrot and between carrot and tomato, grape, and kiwifruit was evaluated with MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3186">114</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 63</a>). The synonymous mutation rate (<i>k</i><sub>s</sub>) and fourfold-degenerate transversion rate were calculated using the HKY model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Hasegawa, M., Kishino, H. &amp; Yano, T. Dating of the human–ape splitting by a molecular clock of mitochondrial DNA. J. Mol. Evol. 22, 160–174 (1985)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3197">115</a></sup>.</p><p>The paleopolyploid history was determined as described by Salse<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Salse, J. et al. Reconstruction of monocotelydoneous proto-chromosomes reveals faster evolution in plants than in animals. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 14908–14913 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3205">33</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM299">Supplementary Figs. 35</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM299">36</a>). Grape–carrot syntenic blocks descending from the seven ancestral chromosomes were detected in carrot as compared with grape, kiwifruit, tomato, and coffee (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM299">Supplementary Fig. 37</a>).</p><p>Divergence and WGD time points in the carrot and tomato genomes were estimated using a method described by Vanneste <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Vanneste, K., Baele, G., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Analysis of 41 plant genomes supports a wave of successful genome duplications in association with the Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary. Genome Res. 24, 1334–1347 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3227">30</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 64</a>).</p><p>The comparative analysis with the horseweed genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Peng, Y. et al. De novo genome assembly of the economically important weed horseweed using integrated data from multiple sequencing platforms. Plant Physiol. 166, 1241–1254 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3237">9</a></sup> used the same gene prediction pipeline described earlier. In total 38,199 genes were predicted and clustered using OrthoMCL to find single-copy gene families across 14 species. A maximum-likelihood tree was reconstructed on the basis of the fourfold-degenerate sites from the 963 single-copy gene families. Reciprocal best BLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Voorrips, R.E. MapChart: software for the graphical presentation of linkage maps and QTLs. J. Hered. 93, 77–78 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3241">69</a></sup> hits within horseweed or between horseweed and other species were used to calculate the paralog/ortholog gene divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM298">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>).</p><p>We collected all syntenic blocks containing genes associated with the Dc-α, Dc-β, and Dc-γ WGD events (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 65</a>). FUNC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Prüfer, K. et al. FUNC: a package for detecting significant associations between gene sets and ontological annotations. BMC Bioinformatics 8, 41 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3254">116</a></sup> was used to carry out a hypergeometric test to identify GO categories with over-representation or under-representation of Dc-α WGD retained and tandem duplicated genes.</p><p>See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a> for additional details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Regulatory and resistance genes: gene family analysis.</h3><p>We used PlantTFcat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Dai, X., Sinharoy, S., Udvardi, M. &amp; Zhao, P.-X. PlantTFcat: an online plant transcription factor and transcriptional regulator categorization and analysis tool. BMC Bioinformatics 14, 321 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3272">117</a></sup> to annotate possible candidate transcription factors, transcription regulators, and chromatin regulators, collectively referred to as regulatory genes. Eleven genomes, including <i>D. carota</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>S. tuberosum</i>, <i>Coffea canephora</i>, <i>Actinidia chinensis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Prunus persica</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, and <i>O. sativa</i>, were screened and filtered for InterPro domains specific for each regulatory gene family.</p><p>Predicted regulatory gene classes were grouped with OrthoMCL as described. We then carried out a detailed analysis of expanded carrot regulatory gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM299">Supplementary Fig. 38</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 66–79</a>). See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a> for the classification of duplication modes of each regulatory gene. For phylogenetic analysis, multiple-sequence alignments with complete protein sequence were conducted using Clustal W<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Larkin, M.A. et al. Clustal W and Clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics 23, 2947–2948 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3323">118</a></sup> with default parameters. Phylogenetic trees were constructed using the neighbor-joining method, with pairwise deletion, using MEGA6 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725–2729 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3326">84</a>).</p><p>MATRIX-R<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Sanseverino, W. et al. PRGdb 2.0: towards a community-based database model for the analysis of R-genes in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, D1167–D1171 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3333">38</a></sup> was used to annotate and classify R genes from nine species, including <i>D. carota</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>S. tuberosum</i>, <i>C. canephora</i>, <i>Capsicum annuum</i>, <i>A. chinensis</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, and <i>O. sativa</i>. Proteins identified via hidden Markov model (HMM) profiling were further analyzed using InterProScan version 5.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3364">119</a>) for conserved domains and motifs characteristic of R proteins (NBS, LRR, TIR, kinase, serine/threonine).</p><p>See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a> for additional details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">A candidate gene controlling carotenoid accumulation.</h3><p>Mapping populations, 97837 (<i>n</i> = 253) and 70796 (<i>n</i> = 285), were used to study the <i>Y</i> locus that regulates carotenoid accumulation in carrot root, where 97837 was derived from an intercross between yellow- and white-rooted cultivars and 70796 was derived from a cross between a dark orange inbred carrot and a wild white-rooted carrot (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM299">Supplementary Figs. 39–41</a>). Carotenoids were quantified as described by Simon and Wolff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Simon, P.W. &amp; Wolff, X.Y. Carotenes in typical and dark orange carrots. J. Agric. Food Chem. 35, 1017–1022 (1987)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3394">120</a></sup> and Simon <i>et al</i>.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Simon, P.W., Wolff, X.Y., Peterson, C.E. &amp; Kammerlohr, D.S. High carotene mass carrot population. HortScience 24, 174–175 (1989)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3402">121</a></sup>.</p><p>Analysis of marker–trait associations was carried out with molecular markers considered as fixed effects in a linear model implemented in the GLM function of TASSEL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Bradbury, P.J. et al. TASSEL: software for association mapping of complex traits in diverse samples. Bioinformatics 23, 2633–2635 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3409">72</a></sup>. The primers used for fine-mapping are reported in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 80</a>. Genome assembly v2.0 was used as a reference to identify marker locations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Tables 81 and 82</a>). The genome-wide significance threshold was determined by the Bonferroni method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 122" title="Bland, J.M. &amp; Altman, D.G. Multiple significance tests: the Bonferroni method. Br. Med. J. 310, 170 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR122" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3419">122</a></sup>. QTL analysis for population 70796 used R package qtl<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 123" title="Broman, K.W. &amp; Sen, S. A Guide to QTL Mapping with R/qtl (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR123" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3423">123</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM300">Supplementary Table 83</a>).</p><p>Resequencing of polymorphisms and phenotypes were used to identify the haplotype block associated with pigmented versus non-pigmented roots. SNPs covering the region associated with high carotenoid accumulation were loaded into TASSEL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Bradbury, P.J. et al. TASSEL: software for association mapping of complex traits in diverse samples. Bioinformatics 23, 2633–2635 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3434">72</a></sup> and manually inspected to identify the start and end of the haplotype block. Sequence from the haplotype block and its flanking sequences were used for haplotype network analysis with PopArt v1.7 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 124" title="Leigh, J.W. &amp; Bryant, D. POPART: full-feature software for haplotype network construction. Methods Ecol. Evol. 6, 1110–1116 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR124" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3437">124</a>).</p><p>Haploview v4.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 125" title="Barrett, J.C., Fry, B., Maller, J. &amp; Daly, M.J. Haploview: analysis and visualization of LD and haplotype maps. Bioinformatics 21, 263–265 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR125" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3443">125</a>) was used to calculate and visualize LD in the candidate region. <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> analysis of 1,393,431 original filtered SNPs was conducted pairwise between each of the 35 resequenced genotypes using VCFTools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 126" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR126" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3451">126</a></sup> with default parameters. The top 1% of <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values were determined and visualized by a custom Perl script (cp2; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#Sec24">URLs</a>). Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) was estimated in TASSEL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Bradbury, P.J. et al. TASSEL: software for association mapping of complex traits in diverse samples. Bioinformatics 23, 2633–2635 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3466">72</a></sup> as described by Nei and Lin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Nei, M. &amp; Li, W.-H. Mathematical model for studying genetic variation in terms of restriction endonucleases. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 76, 5269–5273 (1979)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3470">25</a></sup>.</p><p>See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3565#MOESM295">Supplementary Note</a> for additional details.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Gene expression analysis.</h3><p>Root tissue was collected from population 97837 plants with yellow (<i>yyY</i><sub>2</sub><i>Y</i><sub>2</sub>) and white (<i>YYY</i><sub>2</sub><i>Y</i><sub>2</sub>) genotypes, with two biological replicates per genotype, 80 d after planting. Root tissue was collected from population 70796 plants with dark orange (yyy<sub>2</sub>y<sub>2</sub>) and pale orange (<i>YYy</i><sub>2</sub><i>y</i><sub>2</sub>) genotypes, with three biological replicates, 100 d after planting. Total RNA was extracted from whole-root tissue using the TRIzol Plus RNA Purification kit. RNA quantity and integrity were confirmed with an Experion RNA StdSens Analysis kit. All samples had RQI values above 8.0. Paired-end libraries (insert size of 133 nt) were sequenced on Illumina HiSeq 2000 lanes (2 × 100-nt reads).</p><p>Filtered reads were aligned to the v2.0 genome assembly using TopHat v2.0.12 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3517">78</a>). The aligned read files were processed by Cufflinks v2.2.1 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3565#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d2836e3520">93</a>). Testing for differential expression was done at the level of genes, isoforms, and promoters.
